Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1900 (Focus on the decade from 1890 to 1900. As background, briefly mention significant laws and events from earlier periods, particularly those with substantial influence on the situation during this decade.)

        Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance, 1948

        (Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, 1948: 7-8, Sections I-IV)

        In June 1948, the Legislative Council passed the Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance. The Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, originally named the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union,‚Äù was established in 1915. In 1948, it was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Churches Union‚Äù and applied for corporate status from the government.

        China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance, 1949

        (HK Legislative Council, 1949: 146-147)

        In April 1949, the Legislative Council passed the China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance. The China Peniel Missionary Society had been established in Hong Kong in 1914, and the ordinance granted the society corporate status.

        1. Hong Kong Under Japanese Occupation (Liu Shuyong 2016: 291-304; Carroll 2007: 119-126)

          1941 marked the centenary of Hong Kong as a British colony, but the colony found itself squeezed between three empires: Britain, China, and Japan. In September 1939, Germany invaded Poland, prompting Britain to declare war on Germany, and both the British government and the colonial administration began focusing on Hong Kong‚Äôs defense. In July 1939, the government mandated all European male British subjects aged 18-55 to join the Defence Reserve. In the summer of 1940, air raid shelters were constructed, and large numbers of British women and children were evacuated to Australia. By July 1941, Japanese assets in Hong Kong were frozen.

          China‚Äôs major cities had fallen to Japanese forces, and the Nationalist government had relocated to Chongqing in the southwestern rear. By early 1941, Hong Kong‚Äôs population exceeded 1.5 million, a 50% increase from 1 million in 1937.

          On December 8, 1941, Japanese bombers attacked Hong Kong, Malaya, Pearl Harbor, and the Philippines, triggering the Pacific War. Japanese forces swiftly advanced through the New Territories into Kowloon, distributing leaflets calling on the Chinese in Hong Kong to overthrow British colonial rule. The British retreated to Hong Kong Island, but defense efforts lasted only 17 days. On December 25, 1941, Governor Mark Aitchison Young surrendered unconditionally to Japanese forces. Over 1,500 defenders were killed or went missing, with civilian casualties possibly reaching 4,000.

          The Japanese occupation lasted until August 30, 1945, spanning three years and eight months. The Japanese proclaimed the liberation of Hong Kong as part of their ‚ÄúGreater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere,‚Äù but in reality, they turned the city into a Japanese colony. British civilians were interned in Stanley Prison Camp, and military prisoners were held in the former British barracks in Sham Shui Po. To reduce the population, refugees who had fled south from China were ordered to ‚Äúreturn home,‚Äù reducing Hong Kong‚Äôs population from 1.5 million to 1 million within a year, and further to under 600,000 by Japan‚Äôs surrender in 1945.

          During the occupation, the Japanese implemented a ‚Äúrule by Chinese for Chinese‚Äù policy, forcing many local elites to collaborate by joining the Chinese Representative Council and Chinese Cooperative Council (together known as the ‚ÄúTwo Councils‚Äù). Meanwhile, Japanese military police committed numerous atrocities, including the killing of at least 10,000 civilians, with many others subjected to torture, rape, or permanent injury.

          Despite Japanese rule, Hong Kong became a base for anti-Japanese guerrilla activities. In January 1942, the Chinese Communist South China Work Committee initiated guerrilla warfare in the New Territories, forming the Hong Kong and Kowloon Independent Brigade (East River Column). The Nationalists resumed underground operations by late 1942. Members of the Volunteer Defence Corps who escaped from Sham Shui Po also led the British Army Aid Group, helping European and Chinese residents escape Hong Kong and rescuing Allied pilots shot down by Japanese forces. Cooperation between Nationalists, Communists, and British forces against the Japanese marked a rare moment of unity.

          ‚Äî

          1. Post-War Reclamation and Reconstruction of Hong Kong (Carroll 2007: 126-135; Tsang 1988)

            In January 1942, with U.S. President Franklin Roosevelt‚Äôs support, Chiang Kai-shek became the supreme commander of Allied forces in the China-Burma-India theater. Chiang hoped China‚Äôs elevated international status would aid the abolition of unequal treaties and extraterritorial rights and allow Hong Kong to be reclaimed from Britain. Roosevelt was initially supportive of returning Hong Kong to China. By mid-1942, the British Colonial Office briefly considered this possibility but reversed its stance by 1943, determined to retain Hong Kong after the war. By summer 1945, with Allied victory imminent, Winston Churchill staunchly defended British sovereignty over Hong Kong.

            On August 14, 1945, Japan announced its unconditional surrender. With Nationalist forces nearing Guangzhou, Britain dispatched Rear Admiral Cecil Harcourt to preemptively occupy Hong Kong. Recognizing British reluctance to relinquish Hong Kong, Chiang chose not to jeopardize Sino-British relations, given his reliance on Anglo-American support for his post-war aspirations. With Roosevelt‚Äôs death in April 1945 and his successor Harry Truman adopting a softer stance, Chiang authorized British officials to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Hong Kong after Chiang received Japan‚Äôs surrender in the China theater. On September 16, Harcourt formally accepted Japan‚Äôs surrender in a ceremony attended by Chinese and American officials. After three years and eight months of occupation, Hong Kong resumed its status as a British colony and began post-war reconstruction.

            Hong Kong‚Äôs population surged after the war, reaching 1 million by early 1946. On May 1, Governor Mark Young returned to Hong Kong, proposing the most radical constitutional reforms in its history. He advocated for greater political representation for Chinese residents to strengthen their acceptance of British rule. Key proposals included increasing the number of Chinese members in the Executive and Legislative Councils and establishing a City Council, with two-thirds of its members elected by Chinese voters. Young also promoted government localization, replacing European officials with Chinese personnel. These reforms aimed to transform Hong Kong into a city-state within the British Empire and Commonwealth.

            However, Young‚Äôs plans faced opposition. His successor, Governor Alexander Grantham, appointed in July 1947, doubted Hong Kong‚Äôs potential for independence. While Chinese residents preferred British rule, Grantham feared that if China resolutely demanded Hong Kong‚Äôs return, the situation would be dire. The proposals also lacked support among British officials and Chinese and Western businessmen in Hong Kong. The British Foreign Office worried about provoking China (whether under Nationalist or Communist rule), while local reforms threatened colonial officials‚Äô authority.

            Beyond constitutional reforms, Young successfully repealed discriminatory ordinances, including the 1904 and 1918 Peak District Reservation Ordinances and a 1919 ordinance reserving Cheung Chau for European use. However, bridging the gap and addressing inequalities between Chinese and Europeans in the colony remained a long-term challenge.

            ‚Äî

            1. Hong Kong During the Chinese Civil War (Carroll 2007: 135-138; He Bixiao 2018: 65-71)

              After World War II, the Nationalists and Communists resumed their struggle for legitimacy, leading to the outbreak of civil war in 1946. Despite their military superiority, the Nationalists suffered defeats as key cities such as Shijiazhuang, Jinan, Changchun, Zhengzhou, and Shenyang were ‚Äúliberated‚Äù between 1947 and 1948. By early 1949, Tianjin and Beiping (Beijing) also fell to Communist forces. Cities like Nanjing, Hangzhou, Wuhan, Xi‚Äôan, and Shanghai followed in April and May, with Changsha and Fuzhou falling by September. On October 1, 1949, the People‚Äôs Republic of China was established in Beijing, and by mid-October, Guangzhou and Xiamen were also ‚Äúliberated.‚Äù The Nationalists retreated to Taiwan, establishing the division between the two sides of the Taiwan Strait.

              As major cities in China fell to the Communists, refugees flooded into British Hong Kong, seeking to escape Communist rule. Between 1947 and 1949, Hong Kong‚Äôs population grew from 1.8 million to 2.3 million. Facing political instability and an influx of refugees, the Hong Kong government introduced the Registration of Persons Ordinance and the Deportation of Undesirable Persons Ordinance, both passed by the Legislative Council in August 1949. The former required residents and migrants to obtain identity cards, while the latter targeted 14 categories of ‚Äúundesirable persons,‚Äù allowing summary deportations.

              By October 17, 1949, the People‚Äôs Liberation Army halted at the Hong Kong border. Whether and when Hong Kong would be ‚Äúliberated‚Äù remained uncertain until early 1950, casting a shadow over its future.

              Meanwhile, the Chinese Communist Party actively rebuilt its networks in Hong Kong after the war. The colonial government agreed to negotiations, allowing the CCP to establish a liaison office in Hong Kong. In September 1946, the CCP Guangdong Regional Committee founded Ta Teh Institute, a hub for leftist intellectuals and democrats. In 1947, the CCP established its Hong Kong Bureau, governing areas such as Guangdong, Guangxi, southwestern Fujian, southern Yunnan, and southern Hunan and Jiangxi provinces. Renamed the South China Bureau in early 1949, the CCP had developed a comprehensive underground network in Hong Kong by this time. In June 1948, Governor Grantham sent a confidential report to the Colonial Office detailing the CCP‚Äôs networks, including news agencies, bookstores, labor organizations, and social welfare institutions. In February 1949, the Hong Kong government closed Ta Teh Institute to curb Communist influence. Despite this, the CCP used Hong Kong‚Äôs free environment to facilitate the transport of key figures to Beijing for the first Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. Hong Kong became a critical center for the CCP‚Äôs overseas united front strategy.

        Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance, 1948

        (Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, 1948: 7-8, Sections I-IV)

        In June 1948, the Legislative Council passed the Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance. The Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, originally named the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union,‚Äù was established in 1915. In 1948, it was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Churches Union‚Äù and applied for corporate status from the government.

        China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance, 1949

        (HK Legislative Council, 1949: 146-147)

        In April 1949, the Legislative Council passed the China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance. The China Peniel Missionary Society had been established in Hong Kong in 1914, and the ordinance granted the society corporate status.

        1. Hong Kong Under Japanese Occupation (Liu Shuyong 2016: 291-304; Carroll 2007: 119-126)

          1941 marked the centenary of Hong Kong as a British colony, but the colony found itself squeezed between three empires: Britain, China, and Japan. In September 1939, Germany invaded Poland, prompting Britain to declare war on Germany, and both the British government and the colonial administration began focusing on Hong Kong‚Äôs defense. In July 1939, the government mandated all European male British subjects aged 18-55 to join the Defence Reserve. In the summer of 1940, air raid shelters were constructed, and large numbers of British women and children were evacuated to Australia. By July 1941, Japanese assets in Hong Kong were frozen.

          China‚Äôs major cities had fallen to Japanese forces, and the Nationalist government had relocated to Chongqing in the southwestern rear. By early 1941, Hong Kong‚Äôs population exceeded 1.5 million, a 50% increase from 1 million in 1937.

          On December 8, 1941, Japanese bombers attacked Hong Kong, Malaya, Pearl Harbor, and the Philippines, triggering the Pacific War. Japanese forces swiftly advanced through the New Territories into Kowloon, distributing leaflets calling on the Chinese in Hong Kong to overthrow British colonial rule. The British retreated to Hong Kong Island, but defense efforts lasted only 17 days. On December 25, 1941, Governor Mark Aitchison Young surrendered unconditionally to Japanese forces. Over 1,500 defenders were killed or went missing, with civilian casualties possibly reaching 4,000.

          The Japanese occupation lasted until August 30, 1945, spanning three years and eight months. The Japanese proclaimed the liberation of Hong Kong as part of their ‚ÄúGreater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere,‚Äù but in reality, they turned the city into a Japanese colony. British civilians were interned in Stanley Prison Camp, and military prisoners were held in the former British barracks in Sham Shui Po. To reduce the population, refugees who had fled south from China were ordered to ‚Äúreturn home,‚Äù reducing Hong Kong‚Äôs population from 1.5 million to 1 million within a year, and further to under 600,000 by Japan‚Äôs surrender in 1945.

          During the occupation, the Japanese implemented a ‚Äúrule by Chinese for Chinese‚Äù policy, forcing many local elites to collaborate by joining the Chinese Representative Council and Chinese Cooperative Council (together known as the ‚ÄúTwo Councils‚Äù). Meanwhile, Japanese military police committed numerous atrocities, including the killing of at least 10,000 civilians, with many others subjected to torture, rape, or permanent injury.

          Despite Japanese rule, Hong Kong became a base for anti-Japanese guerrilla activities. In January 1942, the Chinese Communist South China Work Committee initiated guerrilla warfare in the New Territories, forming the Hong Kong and Kowloon Independent Brigade (East River Column). The Nationalists resumed underground operations by late 1942. Members of the Volunteer Defence Corps who escaped from Sham Shui Po also led the British Army Aid Group, helping European and Chinese residents escape Hong Kong and rescuing Allied pilots shot down by Japanese forces. Cooperation between Nationalists, Communists, and British forces against the Japanese marked a rare moment of unity.

          ‚Äî

          1. Post-War Reclamation and Reconstruction of Hong Kong (Carroll 2007: 126-135; Tsang 1988)

            In January 1942, with U.S. President Franklin Roosevelt‚Äôs support, Chiang Kai-shek became the supreme commander of Allied forces in the China-Burma-India theater. Chiang hoped China‚Äôs elevated international status would aid the abolition of unequal treaties and extraterritorial rights and allow Hong Kong to be reclaimed from Britain. Roosevelt was initially supportive of returning Hong Kong to China. By mid-1942, the British Colonial Office briefly considered this possibility but reversed its stance by 1943, determined to retain Hong Kong after the war. By summer 1945, with Allied victory imminent, Winston Churchill staunchly defended British sovereignty over Hong Kong.

            On August 14, 1945, Japan announced its unconditional surrender. With Nationalist forces nearing Guangzhou, Britain dispatched Rear Admiral Cecil Harcourt to preemptively occupy Hong Kong. Recognizing British reluctance to relinquish Hong Kong, Chiang chose not to jeopardize Sino-British relations, given his reliance on Anglo-American support for his post-war aspirations. With Roosevelt‚Äôs death in April 1945 and his successor Harry Truman adopting a softer stance, Chiang authorized British officials to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Hong Kong after Chiang received Japan‚Äôs surrender in the China theater. On September 16, Harcourt formally accepted Japan‚Äôs surrender in a ceremony attended by Chinese and American officials. After three years and eight months of occupation, Hong Kong resumed its status as a British colony and began post-war reconstruction.

            Hong Kong‚Äôs population surged after the war, reaching 1 million by early 1946. On May 1, Governor Mark Young returned to Hong Kong, proposing the most radical constitutional reforms in its history. He advocated for greater political representation for Chinese residents to strengthen their acceptance of British rule. Key proposals included increasing the number of Chinese members in the Executive and Legislative Councils and establishing a City Council, with two-thirds of its members elected by Chinese voters. Young also promoted government localization, replacing European officials with Chinese personnel. These reforms aimed to transform Hong Kong into a city-state within the British Empire and Commonwealth.

            However, Young‚Äôs plans faced opposition. His successor, Governor Alexander Grantham, appointed in July 1947, doubted Hong Kong‚Äôs potential for independence. While Chinese residents preferred British rule, Grantham feared that if China resolutely demanded Hong Kong‚Äôs return, the situation would be dire. The proposals also lacked support among British officials and Chinese and Western businessmen in Hong Kong. The British Foreign Office worried about provoking China (whether under Nationalist or Communist rule), while local reforms threatened colonial officials‚Äô authority.

            Beyond constitutional reforms, Young successfully repealed discriminatory ordinances, including the 1904 and 1918 Peak District Reservation Ordinances and a 1919 ordinance reserving Cheung Chau for European use. However, bridging the gap and addressing inequalities between Chinese and Europeans in the colony remained a long-term challenge.

            ‚Äî

            1. Hong Kong During the Chinese Civil War (Carroll 2007: 135-138; He Bixiao 2018: 65-71)

              After World War II, the Nationalists and Communists resumed their struggle for legitimacy, leading to the outbreak of civil war in 1946. Despite their military superiority, the Nationalists suffered defeats as key cities such as Shijiazhuang, Jinan, Changchun, Zhengzhou, and Shenyang were ‚Äúliberated‚Äù between 1947 and 1948. By early 1949, Tianjin and Beiping (Beijing) also fell to Communist forces. Cities like Nanjing, Hangzhou, Wuhan, Xi‚Äôan, and Shanghai followed in April and May, with Changsha and Fuzhou falling by September. On October 1, 1949, the People‚Äôs Republic of China was established in Beijing, and by mid-October, Guangzhou and Xiamen were also ‚Äúliberated.‚Äù The Nationalists retreated to Taiwan, establishing the division between the two sides of the Taiwan Strait.

              As major cities in China fell to the Communists, refugees flooded into British Hong Kong, seeking to escape Communist rule. Between 1947 and 1949, Hong Kong‚Äôs population grew from 1.8 million to 2.3 million. Facing political instability and an influx of refugees, the Hong Kong government introduced the Registration of Persons Ordinance and the Deportation of Undesirable Persons Ordinance, both passed by the Legislative Council in August 1949. The former required residents and migrants to obtain identity cards, while the latter targeted 14 categories of ‚Äúundesirable persons,‚Äù allowing summary deportations.

              By October 17, 1949, the People‚Äôs Liberation Army halted at the Hong Kong border. Whether and when Hong Kong would be ‚Äúliberated‚Äù remained uncertain until early 1950, casting a shadow over its future.

              Meanwhile, the Chinese Communist Party actively rebuilt its networks in Hong Kong after the war. The colonial government agreed to negotiations, allowing the CCP to establish a liaison office in Hong Kong. In September 1946, the CCP Guangdong Regional Committee founded Ta Teh Institute, a hub for leftist intellectuals and democrats. In 1947, the CCP established its Hong Kong Bureau, governing areas such as Guangdong, Guangxi, southwestern Fujian, southern Yunnan, and southern Hunan and Jiangxi provinces. Renamed the South China Bureau in early 1949, the CCP had developed a comprehensive underground network in Hong Kong by this time. In June 1948, Governor Grantham sent a confidential report to the Colonial Office detailing the CCP‚Äôs networks, including news agencies, bookstores, labor organizations, and social welfare institutions. In February 1949, the Hong Kong government closed Ta Teh Institute to curb Communist influence. Despite this, the CCP used Hong Kong‚Äôs free environment to facilitate the transport of key figures to Beijing for the first Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. Hong Kong became a critical center for the CCP‚Äôs overseas united front strategy.

        Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance, 1948

        (Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, 1948: 7-8, Sections I-IV)

        In June 1948, the Legislative Council passed the Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance. The Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, originally named the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union,‚Äù was established in 1915. In 1948, it was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Churches Union‚Äù and applied for corporate status from the government.

        China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance, 1949

        (HK Legislative Council, 1949: 146-147)

        In April 1949, the Legislative Council passed the China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance. The China Peniel Missionary Society had been established in Hong Kong in 1914, and the ordinance granted the society corporate status.

        1. Hong Kong Under Japanese Occupation (Liu Shuyong 2016: 291-304; Carroll 2007: 119-126)

          1941 marked the centenary of Hong Kong as a British colony, but the colony found itself squeezed between three empires: Britain, China, and Japan. In September 1939, Germany invaded Poland, prompting Britain to declare war on Germany, and both the British government and the colonial administration began focusing on Hong Kong‚Äôs defense. In July 1939, the government mandated all European male British subjects aged 18-55 to join the Defence Reserve. In the summer of 1940, air raid shelters were constructed, and large numbers of British women and children were evacuated to Australia. By July 1941, Japanese assets in Hong Kong were frozen.

          China‚Äôs major cities had fallen to Japanese forces, and the Nationalist government had relocated to Chongqing in the southwestern rear. By early 1941, Hong Kong‚Äôs population exceeded 1.5 million, a 50% increase from 1 million in 1937.

          On December 8, 1941, Japanese bombers attacked Hong Kong, Malaya, Pearl Harbor, and the Philippines, triggering the Pacific War. Japanese forces swiftly advanced through the New Territories into Kowloon, distributing leaflets calling on the Chinese in Hong Kong to overthrow British colonial rule. The British retreated to Hong Kong Island, but defense efforts lasted only 17 days. On December 25, 1941, Governor Mark Aitchison Young surrendered unconditionally to Japanese forces. Over 1,500 defenders were killed or went missing, with civilian casualties possibly reaching 4,000.

          The Japanese occupation lasted until August 30, 1945, spanning three years and eight months. The Japanese proclaimed the liberation of Hong Kong as part of their ‚ÄúGreater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere,‚Äù but in reality, they turned the city into a Japanese colony. British civilians were interned in Stanley Prison Camp, and military prisoners were held in the former British barracks in Sham Shui Po. To reduce the population, refugees who had fled south from China were ordered to ‚Äúreturn home,‚Äù reducing Hong Kong‚Äôs population from 1.5 million to 1 million within a year, and further to under 600,000 by Japan‚Äôs surrender in 1945.

          During the occupation, the Japanese implemented a ‚Äúrule by Chinese for Chinese‚Äù policy, forcing many local elites to collaborate by joining the Chinese Representative Council and Chinese Cooperative Council (together known as the ‚ÄúTwo Councils‚Äù). Meanwhile, Japanese military police committed numerous atrocities, including the killing of at least 10,000 civilians, with many others subjected to torture, rape, or permanent injury.

          Despite Japanese rule, Hong Kong became a base for anti-Japanese guerrilla activities. In January 1942, the Chinese Communist South China Work Committee initiated guerrilla warfare in the New Territories, forming the Hong Kong and Kowloon Independent Brigade (East River Column). The Nationalists resumed underground operations by late 1942. Members of the Volunteer Defence Corps who escaped from Sham Shui Po also led the British Army Aid Group, helping European and Chinese residents escape Hong Kong and rescuing Allied pilots shot down by Japanese forces. Cooperation between Nationalists, Communists, and British forces against the Japanese marked a rare moment of unity.

          ‚Äî

          1. Post-War Reclamation and Reconstruction of Hong Kong (Carroll 2007: 126-135; Tsang 1988)

            In January 1942, with U.S. President Franklin Roosevelt‚Äôs support, Chiang Kai-shek became the supreme commander of Allied forces in the China-Burma-India theater. Chiang hoped China‚Äôs elevated international status would aid the abolition of unequal treaties and extraterritorial rights and allow Hong Kong to be reclaimed from Britain. Roosevelt was initially supportive of returning Hong Kong to China. By mid-1942, the British Colonial Office briefly considered this possibility but reversed its stance by 1943, determined to retain Hong Kong after the war. By summer 1945, with Allied victory imminent, Winston Churchill staunchly defended British sovereignty over Hong Kong.

            On August 14, 1945, Japan announced its unconditional surrender. With Nationalist forces nearing Guangzhou, Britain dispatched Rear Admiral Cecil Harcourt to preemptively occupy Hong Kong. Recognizing British reluctance to relinquish Hong Kong, Chiang chose not to jeopardize Sino-British relations, given his reliance on Anglo-American support for his post-war aspirations. With Roosevelt‚Äôs death in April 1945 and his successor Harry Truman adopting a softer stance, Chiang authorized British officials to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Hong Kong after Chiang received Japan‚Äôs surrender in the China theater. On September 16, Harcourt formally accepted Japan‚Äôs surrender in a ceremony attended by Chinese and American officials. After three years and eight months of occupation, Hong Kong resumed its status as a British colony and began post-war reconstruction.

            Hong Kong‚Äôs population surged after the war, reaching 1 million by early 1946. On May 1, Governor Mark Young returned to Hong Kong, proposing the most radical constitutional reforms in its history. He advocated for greater political representation for Chinese residents to strengthen their acceptance of British rule. Key proposals included increasing the number of Chinese members in the Executive and Legislative Councils and establishing a City Council, with two-thirds of its members elected by Chinese voters. Young also promoted government localization, replacing European officials with Chinese personnel. These reforms aimed to transform Hong Kong into a city-state within the British Empire and Commonwealth.

            However, Young‚Äôs plans faced opposition. His successor, Governor Alexander Grantham, appointed in July 1947, doubted Hong Kong‚Äôs potential for independence. While Chinese residents preferred British rule, Grantham feared that if China resolutely demanded Hong Kong‚Äôs return, the situation would be dire. The proposals also lacked support among British officials and Chinese and Western businessmen in Hong Kong. The British Foreign Office worried about provoking China (whether under Nationalist or Communist rule), while local reforms threatened colonial officials‚Äô authority.

            Beyond constitutional reforms, Young successfully repealed discriminatory ordinances, including the 1904 and 1918 Peak District Reservation Ordinances and a 1919 ordinance reserving Cheung Chau for European use. However, bridging the gap and addressing inequalities between Chinese and Europeans in the colony remained a long-term challenge.

            ‚Äî

            1. Hong Kong During the Chinese Civil War (Carroll 2007: 135-138; He Bixiao 2018: 65-71)

              After World War II, the Nationalists and Communists resumed their struggle for legitimacy, leading to the outbreak of civil war in 1946. Despite their military superiority, the Nationalists suffered defeats as key cities such as Shijiazhuang, Jinan, Changchun, Zhengzhou, and Shenyang were ‚Äúliberated‚Äù between 1947 and 1948. By early 1949, Tianjin and Beiping (Beijing) also fell to Communist forces. Cities like Nanjing, Hangzhou, Wuhan, Xi‚Äôan, and Shanghai followed in April and May, with Changsha and Fuzhou falling by September. On October 1, 1949, the People‚Äôs Republic of China was established in Beijing, and by mid-October, Guangzhou and Xiamen were also ‚Äúliberated.‚Äù The Nationalists retreated to Taiwan, establishing the division between the two sides of the Taiwan Strait.

              As major cities in China fell to the Communists, refugees flooded into British Hong Kong, seeking to escape Communist rule. Between 1947 and 1949, Hong Kong‚Äôs population grew from 1.8 million to 2.3 million. Facing political instability and an influx of refugees, the Hong Kong government introduced the Registration of Persons Ordinance and the Deportation of Undesirable Persons Ordinance, both passed by the Legislative Council in August 1949. The former required residents and migrants to obtain identity cards, while the latter targeted 14 categories of ‚Äúundesirable persons,‚Äù allowing summary deportations.

              By October 17, 1949, the People‚Äôs Liberation Army halted at the Hong Kong border. Whether and when Hong Kong would be ‚Äúliberated‚Äù remained uncertain until early 1950, casting a shadow over its future.

              Meanwhile, the Chinese Communist Party actively rebuilt its networks in Hong Kong after the war. The colonial government agreed to negotiations, allowing the CCP to establish a liaison office in Hong Kong. In September 1946, the CCP Guangdong Regional Committee founded Ta Teh Institute, a hub for leftist intellectuals and democrats. In 1947, the CCP established its Hong Kong Bureau, governing areas such as Guangdong, Guangxi, southwestern Fujian, southern Yunnan, and southern Hunan and Jiangxi provinces. Renamed the South China Bureau in early 1949, the CCP had developed a comprehensive underground network in Hong Kong by this time. In June 1948, Governor Grantham sent a confidential report to the Colonial Office detailing the CCP‚Äôs networks, including news agencies, bookstores, labor organizations, and social welfare institutions. In February 1949, the Hong Kong government closed Ta Teh Institute to curb Communist influence. Despite this, the CCP used Hong Kong‚Äôs free environment to facilitate the transport of key figures to Beijing for the first Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. Hong Kong became a critical center for the CCP‚Äôs overseas united front strategy.

        Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance, 1948

        (Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, 1948: 7-8, Sections I-IV)

        In June 1948, the Legislative Council passed the Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union Incorporation Ordinance. The Hong Kong Chinese Churches Union, originally named the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union,‚Äù was established in 1915. In 1948, it was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Chinese Churches Union‚Äù and applied for corporate status from the government.

        China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance, 1949

        (HK Legislative Council, 1949: 146-147)

        In April 1949, the Legislative Council passed the China Peniel Missionary Society Incorporation Ordinance. The China Peniel Missionary Society had been established in Hong Kong in 1914, and the ordinance granted the society corporate status.

        1. Hong Kong Under Japanese Occupation (Liu Shuyong 2016: 291-304; Carroll 2007: 119-126)

          1941 marked the centenary of Hong Kong as a British colony, but the colony found itself squeezed between three empires: Britain, China, and Japan. In September 1939, Germany invaded Poland, prompting Britain to declare war on Germany, and both the British government and the colonial administration began focusing on Hong Kong‚Äôs defense. In July 1939, the government mandated all European male British subjects aged 18-55 to join the Defence Reserve. In the summer of 1940, air raid shelters were constructed, and large numbers of British women and children were evacuated to Australia. By July 1941, Japanese assets in Hong Kong were frozen.

          China‚Äôs major cities had fallen to Japanese forces, and the Nationalist government had relocated to Chongqing in the southwestern rear. By early 1941, Hong Kong‚Äôs population exceeded 1.5 million, a 50% increase from 1 million in 1937.

          On December 8, 1941, Japanese bombers attacked Hong Kong, Malaya, Pearl Harbor, and the Philippines, triggering the Pacific War. Japanese forces swiftly advanced through the New Territories into Kowloon, distributing leaflets calling on the Chinese in Hong Kong to overthrow British colonial rule. The British retreated to Hong Kong Island, but defense efforts lasted only 17 days. On December 25, 1941, Governor Mark Aitchison Young surrendered unconditionally to Japanese forces. Over 1,500 defenders were killed or went missing, with civilian casualties possibly reaching 4,000.

          The Japanese occupation lasted until August 30, 1945, spanning three years and eight months. The Japanese proclaimed the liberation of Hong Kong as part of their ‚ÄúGreater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere,‚Äù but in reality, they turned the city into a Japanese colony. British civilians were interned in Stanley Prison Camp, and military prisoners were held in the former British barracks in Sham Shui Po. To reduce the population, refugees who had fled south from China were ordered to ‚Äúreturn home,‚Äù reducing Hong Kong‚Äôs population from 1.5 million to 1 million within a year, and further to under 600,000 by Japan‚Äôs surrender in 1945.

          During the occupation, the Japanese implemented a ‚Äúrule by Chinese for Chinese‚Äù policy, forcing many local elites to collaborate by joining the Chinese Representative Council and Chinese Cooperative Council (together known as the ‚ÄúTwo Councils‚Äù). Meanwhile, Japanese military police committed numerous atrocities, including the killing of at least 10,000 civilians, with many others subjected to torture, rape, or permanent injury.

          Despite Japanese rule, Hong Kong became a base for anti-Japanese guerrilla activities. In January 1942, the Chinese Communist South China Work Committee initiated guerrilla warfare in the New Territories, forming the Hong Kong and Kowloon Independent Brigade (East River Column). The Nationalists resumed underground operations by late 1942. Members of the Volunteer Defence Corps who escaped from Sham Shui Po also led the British Army Aid Group, helping European and Chinese residents escape Hong Kong and rescuing Allied pilots shot down by Japanese forces. Cooperation between Nationalists, Communists, and British forces against the Japanese marked a rare moment of unity.

          ‚Äî

          1. Post-War Reclamation and Reconstruction of Hong Kong (Carroll 2007: 126-135; Tsang 1988)

            In January 1942, with U.S. President Franklin Roosevelt‚Äôs support, Chiang Kai-shek became the supreme commander of Allied forces in the China-Burma-India theater. Chiang hoped China‚Äôs elevated international status would aid the abolition of unequal treaties and extraterritorial rights and allow Hong Kong to be reclaimed from Britain. Roosevelt was initially supportive of returning Hong Kong to China. By mid-1942, the British Colonial Office briefly considered this possibility but reversed its stance by 1943, determined to retain Hong Kong after the war. By summer 1945, with Allied victory imminent, Winston Churchill staunchly defended British sovereignty over Hong Kong.

            On August 14, 1945, Japan announced its unconditional surrender. With Nationalist forces nearing Guangzhou, Britain dispatched Rear Admiral Cecil Harcourt to preemptively occupy Hong Kong. Recognizing British reluctance to relinquish Hong Kong, Chiang chose not to jeopardize Sino-British relations, given his reliance on Anglo-American support for his post-war aspirations. With Roosevelt‚Äôs death in April 1945 and his successor Harry Truman adopting a softer stance, Chiang authorized British officials to accept Japan‚Äôs surrender in Hong Kong after Chiang received Japan‚Äôs surrender in the China theater. On September 16, Harcourt formally accepted Japan‚Äôs surrender in a ceremony attended by Chinese and American officials. After three years and eight months of occupation, Hong Kong resumed its status as a British colony and began post-war reconstruction.

            Hong Kong‚Äôs population surged after the war, reaching 1 million by early 1946. On May 1, Governor Mark Young returned to Hong Kong, proposing the most radical constitutional reforms in its history. He advocated for greater political representation for Chinese residents to strengthen their acceptance of British rule. Key proposals included increasing the number of Chinese members in the Executive and Legislative Councils and establishing a City Council, with two-thirds of its members elected by Chinese voters. Young also promoted government localization, replacing European officials with Chinese personnel. These reforms aimed to transform Hong Kong into a city-state within the British Empire and Commonwealth.

            However, Young‚Äôs plans faced opposition. His successor, Governor Alexander Grantham, appointed in July 1947, doubted Hong Kong‚Äôs potential for independence. While Chinese residents preferred British rule, Grantham feared that if China resolutely demanded Hong Kong‚Äôs return, the situation would be dire. The proposals also lacked support among British officials and Chinese and Western businessmen in Hong Kong. The British Foreign Office worried about provoking China (whether under Nationalist or Communist rule), while local reforms threatened colonial officials‚Äô authority.

            Beyond constitutional reforms, Young successfully repealed discriminatory ordinances, including the 1904 and 1918 Peak District Reservation Ordinances and a 1919 ordinance reserving Cheung Chau for European use. However, bridging the gap and addressing inequalities between Chinese and Europeans in the colony remained a long-term challenge.

            ‚Äî

            1. Hong Kong During the Chinese Civil War (Carroll 2007: 135-138; He Bixiao 2018: 65-71)

              After World War II, the Nationalists and Communists resumed their struggle for legitimacy, leading to the outbreak of civil war in 1946. Despite their military superiority, the Nationalists suffered defeats as key cities such as Shijiazhuang, Jinan, Changchun, Zhengzhou, and Shenyang were ‚Äúliberated‚Äù between 1947 and 1948. By early 1949, Tianjin and Beiping (Beijing) also fell to Communist forces. Cities like Nanjing, Hangzhou, Wuhan, Xi‚Äôan, and Shanghai followed in April and May, with Changsha and Fuzhou falling by September. On October 1, 1949, the People‚Äôs Republic of China was established in Beijing, and by mid-October, Guangzhou and Xiamen were also ‚Äúliberated.‚Äù The Nationalists retreated to Taiwan, establishing the division between the two sides of the Taiwan Strait.

              As major cities in China fell to the Communists, refugees flooded into British Hong Kong, seeking to escape Communist rule. Between 1947 and 1949, Hong Kong‚Äôs population grew from 1.8 million to 2.3 million. Facing political instability and an influx of refugees, the Hong Kong government introduced the Registration of Persons Ordinance and the Deportation of Undesirable Persons Ordinance, both passed by the Legislative Council in August 1949. The former required residents and migrants to obtain identity cards, while the latter targeted 14 categories of ‚Äúundesirable persons,‚Äù allowing summary deportations.

              By October 17, 1949, the People‚Äôs Liberation Army halted at the Hong Kong border. Whether and when Hong Kong would be ‚Äúliberated‚Äù remained uncertain until early 1950, casting a shadow over its future.

              Meanwhile, the Chinese Communist Party actively rebuilt its networks in Hong Kong after the war. The colonial government agreed to negotiations, allowing the CCP to establish a liaison office in Hong Kong. In September 1946, the CCP Guangdong Regional Committee founded Ta Teh Institute, a hub for leftist intellectuals and democrats. In 1947, the CCP established its Hong Kong Bureau, governing areas such as Guangdong, Guangxi, southwestern Fujian, southern Yunnan, and southern Hunan and Jiangxi provinces. Renamed the South China Bureau in early 1949, the CCP had developed a comprehensive underground network in Hong Kong by this time. In June 1948, Governor Grantham sent a confidential report to the Colonial Office detailing the CCP‚Äôs networks, including news agencies, bookstores, labor organizations, and social welfare institutions. In February 1949, the Hong Kong government closed Ta Teh Institute to curb Communist influence. Despite this, the CCP used Hong Kong‚Äôs free environment to facilitate the transport of key figures to Beijing for the first Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. Hong Kong became a critical center for the CCP‚Äôs overseas united front strategy.
